<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Euclid</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>

<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Ancient Greek mathematician (fl. 300 BC)</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note"><span>Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Euclid_of_Megara" title="Euclid of Megara">Euclid of Megara</a>.</span> <span>For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Euclid_(disambiguation)" title="Euclid (disambiguation)">Euclid (disambiguation)</a>.</span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox biography vcard"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="font-size:125%;"><div class="fn" style="display:inline">Euclid</div></th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-subheader" colspan="2" style="font-size:125%; font-weight:bold;">Îá½ÎºÎ»ÎµÎ¯Î´Î·Ï</td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-image" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jusepe_de_Ribera_-_Euclid_-_2001.26_-_J._Paul_Getty_Museum.jpg"><img alt="Jusepe de Ribera - Euclid - 2001.26 - J. <div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="text-align: center; font-size: 95%;">
<div style="line-height: 1.6em; font-weight: bold; font-size: 100%; text-align: left;"><div><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047488">.mw-parser-output .nobold{font-weight:normal}</style><span class="nobold">Various concepts</span></div></div>
<ul class="mw-collapsible-content" style="font-size: 105%; margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0; line-height: inherit; text-align: left; list-style: none; margin-left: 0;"><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"><div class="plainlist">

<li><a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_theorem" title="Euclid's theorem">Euclid's theorem</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Euclidean_relation" title="Euclidean relation">Euclidean relation</a></li>

</div></li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><b>Scientific career</b></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Fields</th><td class="infobox-data category"><a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">Mathematics</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Influences</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Eudoxus_of_Cnidus" title="Eudoxus of Cnidus">Eudoxus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates_of_Chios" title="Hippocrates of Chios">Hippocrates of Chios</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thales" title="Thales">Thales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theaetetus_(mathematician)" title="Theaetetus (mathematician)">Theaetetus</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Influenced</th><td class="infobox-data">Virtually all subsequent geometry of the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a> and <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsper2010Â§_para._7_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsper2010Â§_para._7-2">[2]</a></sup></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Euclid</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="/juË/: 'u' in 'cute'">juË</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/Éª/: 'i' in 'kit'">Éª</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%95%E1%BD%90%CE%BA%CE%BB%CE%B5%CE%AF%CE%B4%CE%B7%CF%82" title="wikt:Îá½ÎºÎ»ÎµÎ¯Î´Î·Ï">Îá½ÎºÎ»ÎµÎ¯Î´Î·Ï</a></span></span>; <abbr title="floruit ('flourished'Â â known to have been active at a particular time or during a particular period)">fl.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">â300</span> BC) was an ancient Greek <a href="/wiki/Mathematician" title="Mathematician">mathematician</a> active as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geometer" title="Geometer">geometer</a> and logician.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage125_125]_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage125_125]-3">[3]</a></sup> Considered the "father of geometry",<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Summary"_4-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Summary"-4'>[4]</a></sup> he is chiefly known for the <i><a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid's Elements">Elements</a></i> treatise, which established the foundations of <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">geometry</a> that largely dominated the field until the early 19th century. His system, now referred to as <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_geometry" title="Euclidean geometry">Euclidean geometry</a>, involved new innovations in combination with a synthesis of theories from earlier Greek mathematicians, including <a href="/wiki/Eudoxus_of_Cnidus" title="Eudoxus of Cnidus">Eudoxus of Cnidus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates_of_Chios" title="Hippocrates of Chios">Hippocrates of Chios</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thales" title="Thales">Thales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theaetetus_(mathematician)" title="Theaetetus (mathematician)">Theaetetus</a>. With <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Perga" title="Apollonius of Perga">Apollonius of Perga</a>, Euclid is generally considered among the greatest mathematicians of antiquity, and one of the most influential in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_mathematics" title="History of mathematics">history of mathematics</a>.
</p><p>Very little is known of Euclid's life, and most information comes from the philosophers <a href="/wiki/Proclus" title="Proclus">Proclus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pappus_of_Alexandria" title="Pappus of Alexandria">Pappus of Alexandria</a> many centuries later. Until the early <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> he was often mistaken for the earlier philosopher <a href="/wiki/Euclid_of_Megara" title="Euclid of Megara">Euclid of Megara</a>, causing his biography to be substantially revised. It is generally agreed that he spent his career under <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_I_Soter" title="Ptolemy I Soter">Ptolemy I</a> in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> and lived around 300 BC, after <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> and before Archimedes. There is some speculation that Euclid was a student of the <a href="/wiki/Platonic_Academy" title="Platonic Academy">Platonic Academy</a>. Euclid is often regarded as bridging between the earlier Platonic tradition in <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> with the later tradition of Alexandria.
</p><p>In the <i>Elements</i>, Euclid deduced the theorems from a small set of <a href="/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">axioms</a>. He also wrote works on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perspective_(visual)" title="Perspective (visual)">perspective</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conic_section" title="Conic section">conic sections</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spherical_geometry" title="Spherical geometry">spherical geometry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Number_theory" title="Number theory">number theory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rigour#Mathematical_proof" title="Rigour">mathematical rigour</a>. In addition to the <i>Elements</i>, Euclid wrote a central early text in the <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a> field, <i><a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Optics" title="Euclid's Optics">Optics</a></i>, and lesser-known works including <i><a href="/wiki/Data_(Euclid)" title="Data (Euclid)">Data</a></i> and <i>Phaenomena</i>. Euclid's authorship of two other textsâ<i>On Divisions of Figures</i>, <i>Catoptrics</i>âhas been questioned. He is thought to have written many now <a href="#Lost_works">lost works</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_narrative">Traditional narrative</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P._Oxy._I_29.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1032" data-file-width="1694" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/P._Oxy._I_29.jpg/350px-P._Oxy._I_29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/P._Oxy._I_29.jpg/525px-P._Oxy._I_29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/P._Oxy._I_29.jpg/700px-P._Oxy._I_29.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:P._Oxy._I_29.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Papyrus_Oxyrhynchus_29" title="Papyrus Oxyrhynchus 29">papyrus fragment</a> of Euclid's <i>Elements</i> dated to <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">â75â125 AD</span>. Found at <a href="/wiki/Oxyrhynchus" title="Oxyrhynchus">Oxyrhynchus</a>, the diagram accompanies Book II, Proposition 5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler1999210â211_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler1999210â211-5">[5]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The English name 'Euclid' is the anglicized version of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> name <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%95%E1%BD%90%CE%BA%CE%BB%CE%B5%CE%AF%CE%B4%CE%B7%CF%82" title="wikt:Îá½ÎºÎ»ÎµÎ¯Î´Î·Ï">Îá½ÎºÎ»ÎµÎ¯Î´Î·Ï</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"_6-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"-6'>[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[a]</a></sup> It is derived from '<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/eu-#English" title="wikt:eu-">eu-</a>' (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%B5%E1%BD%96#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:Îµá½">Îµá½</a>; 'well') and 'klÃªs' (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/#-ÎºÎ»á¿Ï" title="wikt:">-ÎºÎ»á¿Ï</a>; 'fame'), meaning "renowned, glorious".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE''OED''b_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE''OED''b-9">[8]</a></sup> The word 'Euclid' less commonly also means "a copy of the same",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE''OED''a_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE''OED''a-7">[7]</a></sup> and is sometimes synonymous with 'geometry'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage125_125]_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage125_125]-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Like many ancient Greek mathematicians, Euclid's life is mostly unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeath1981354_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeath1981354-10">[9]</a></sup> He is accepted as the author of four mostly extant treatisesâthe <i><a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid's Elements">Elements</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Optics" title="Euclid's Optics">Optics</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Data_(Euclid)" title="Data (Euclid)">Data</a></i>, <i>Phaenomena</i>âbut besides this, there is nothing known for certain of him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsper2010Â§_para._1_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsper2010Â§_para._1-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[b]</a></sup> The historian <a href="/wiki/Carl_Benjamin_Boyer" title="Carl Benjamin Boyer">Carl Benjamin Boyer</a> has noted irony in that "Considering the fame of the author and of his best seller [the <i>Elements</i>], remarkably little is known of Euclid".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991[httpsarchiveorgdetailshistoryofmathema00boyepage100mode2upviewtheater_100]_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991[httpsarchiveorgdetailshistoryofmathema00boyepage100mode2upviewtheater_100]-14">[12]</a></sup> The traditional narrative mainly follows the 5th century AD account by <a href="/wiki/Proclus" title="Proclus">Proclus</a> in his <i>Commentary on the First Book of Euclid's Elements</i>, as well as a few anecdotes from <a href="/wiki/Pappus_of_Alexandria" title="Pappus of Alexandria">Pappus of Alexandria</a> in the early 4th century.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"_6-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"-6'>[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[c]</a></sup> According to Proclus, Euclid lived after the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="died">d.</abbr>â347</span> BC) and before the mathematician <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">â287</span>Â â c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;">â212</span> BC); specifically, Proclus placed Euclid during the rule of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_I_Soter" title="Ptolemy I Soter">Ptolemy I</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr>Â 305/304â282</span> BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsper2010Â§_para._1_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsper2010Â§_para._1-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeath1981354_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeath1981354-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[d]</a></sup> In his <i>Collection</i>, Pappus indicates that Euclid was active in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, where he founded a mathematical tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsper2010Â§_para._1_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsper2010Â§_para._1-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2020142_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2020142-18">[14]</a></sup> Thus, the traditional outlineâdescribed by the historian Michalis Sialaros as the "dominant view"âholds that Euclid lived around 300 BC in Alexandria while Ptolemy I reigned.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"_6-2'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"-6'>[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Euclid's birthdate is unknown; some scholars estimate around 330<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBall1960[httpsarchiveorgdetailsshortaccountofhi0000ballpage52mode2up_52]_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBall1960[httpsarchiveorgdetailsshortaccountofhi0000ballpage52mode2up_52]-19">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2020141_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2020141-20">[16]</a></sup> or 325 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage125_125]_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage125_125]-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoulding2010125_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoulding2010125-21">[17]</a></sup> but other sources avoid speculating a date entirely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmorynski20082_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmorynski20082-22">[18]</a></sup> It is presumed that he was of Greek descent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBall1960[httpsarchiveorgdetailsshortaccountofhi0000ballpage52mode2up_52]_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBall1960[httpsarchiveorgdetailsshortaccountofhi0000ballpage52mode2up_52]-19">[15]</a></sup> but his birthplace is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991[httpsarchiveorgdetailshistoryofmathema00boyepage100mode2upviewtheater_100]_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991[httpsarchiveorgdetailshistoryofmathema00boyepage100mode2upviewtheater_100]-14">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[e]</a></sup> Proclus held that Euclid followed the <a href="/wiki/Platonism" title="Platonism">Platonic tradition</a>, but there is no definitive confirmation for this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeath1981355_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeath1981355-25">[20]</a></sup> It is unlikely he was contemporary with Plato, so it is often presumed that he was educated by Plato's disciples at the <a href="/wiki/Platonic_Academy" title="Platonic Academy">Platonic Academy</a> in Athens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoulding2010126_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoulding2010126-26">[21]</a></sup> The historian <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Heath_(classicist)" title="Thomas Heath (classicist)">Thomas Heath</a> supported this theory by noting that most capable geometers lived in Athens, which included many of the mathematicians whose work Euclid later built on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeath1908[httpsarchiveorgdetailsbub_gb_UhgPAAAAIAAJpagen13mode2up_2]_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeath1908[httpsarchiveorgdetailsbub_gb_UhgPAAAAIAAJpagen13mode2up_2]-27">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2020147_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2020147-28">[23]</a></sup> The accuracy of these assertions has been questioned by Sialaros,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2020147â148_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2020147â148-29">[24]</a></sup> who stated that Heath's theory "must be treated merely as a conjecture".<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"_6-4'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"-6'>[6]</a></sup> Regardless of his actual attendance at the Platonic academy, the contents of his later work certainly suggest he was familiar with the Platonic geometry tradition, though they also demonstrate no observable influence from <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBall1960[httpsarchiveorgdetailsshortaccountofhi0000ballpage52mode2up_52]_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBall1960[httpsarchiveorgdetailsshortaccountofhi0000ballpage52mode2up_52]-19">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> founded Alexandria in 331 BC, where Euclid would later be active sometime around 300 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage126_126]_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage126_126]-30">[25]</a></sup> The rule of Ptolemy I from 306 BC onwards gave the city a stability which was relatively unique in the Mediterranean, amid the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Diadochi" title="Wars of the Diadochi">chaotic wars over dividing Alexander's empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBall1960[httpsarchiveorgdetailsshortaccountofhi0000ballpage50mode2up_51]_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBall1960[httpsarchiveorgdetailsshortaccountofhi0000ballpage50mode2up_51]-31">[26]</a></sup> Ptolemy began a process of <a href="/wiki/Hellenization" title="Hellenization">hellenization</a> and commissioned numerous constructions, building the massive <a href="/wiki/Musaeum" title="Musaeum">Musaeum</a> institution, which was a leading center of education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991[httpsarchiveorgdetailshistoryofmathema00boyepage100mode2upviewtheater_100]_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991[httpsarchiveorgdetailshistoryofmathema00boyepage100mode2upviewtheater_100]-14">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[f]</a></sup> On the basis of later anecdotes, Euclid is thought to have been among the Musaeum's first scholars and to have founded the Alexandrian school of mathematics there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage126_126]_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage126_126]-30">[25]</a></sup> According to Pappus, the later mathematician <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Perga" title="Apollonius of Perga">Apollonius of Perga</a> was taught there by pupils of Euclid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeath1908[httpsarchiveorgdetailsbub_gb_UhgPAAAAIAAJpagen13mode2up_2]_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeath1908[httpsarchiveorgdetailsbub_gb_UhgPAAAAIAAJpagen13mode2up_2]-27">[22]</a></sup> Euclid's date of death is unknown; it has been estimated that he died <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">â270</span> BC, presumably in Alexandria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage126_126]_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage126_126]-30">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Identity_and_historicity">Identity and historicity</span></h3>
<p>Euclid is often referred to as 'Euclid of Alexandria' to differentiate him from the earlier philosopher <a href="/wiki/Euclid_of_Megara" title="Euclid of Megara">Euclid of Megara</a>, a pupil of <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> who was included in the <a href="/wiki/List_of_speakers_in_Plato%27s_dialogues" title="List of speakers in Plato's dialogues">dialogues of Plato</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"_6-5'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"-6'>[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmorynski20082_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmorynski20082-22">[18]</a></sup> Historically, medieval scholars frequently confused the mathematician and philosopher, mistakenly referring to the former in Latin as 'Megarensis' (<abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;">â</span>'of Megara').<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaisbakWaerden2021Â§_"Life"_34-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaisbakWaerden2021Â§_"Life"-34'>[28]</a></sup> As a result, biographical information on the mathematician Euclid was long conflated with the lives of both Euclid of Alexandria and Euclid of Megara.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"_6-6'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"-6'>[6]</a></sup> The only scholar of antiquity known to have confused the mathematician and philosopher was <a href="/wiki/Valerius_Maximus" title="Valerius Maximus">Valerius Maximus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoulding2010120_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoulding2010120-35">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[g]</a></sup> However, this mistaken identification was relayed by many anonymous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> sources and the Renaissance scholars <a href="/wiki/Campanus_of_Novara" title="Campanus of Novara">Campanus of Novara</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Metochites" title="Theodore Metochites">Theodore Metochites</a>, the latter of whom included it in a 1842 translation of the <i>Elements</i> printed by <a href="/wiki/Erhard_Ratdolt" title="Erhard Ratdolt">Erhard Ratdolt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoulding2010120_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoulding2010120-35">[29]</a></sup> After the mathematician <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bartolomeo_Zamberti&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bartolomeo Zamberti (page does not exist)">Bartolomeo Zamberti</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">Â [<a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bartolomeo_Zamberti" title="fr:Bartolomeo Zamberti">fr</a>]</span> (1473â1539) affirmed this presumption in his 1505 translation, all subsequent publications passed on this identification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoulding2010120_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoulding2010120-35">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[h]</a></sup> Later Renaissance scholars, particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_Ramus" title="Peter Ramus">Peter Ramus</a>, reevaluated this claim, proving it false via issues in chronology and contradiction in early sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoulding2010120_35-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoulding2010120-35">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Arab sources written many centuries after his death give vast amounts of information concerning Euclid's life, but are completely unverifiable.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"_6-7'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"-6'>[6]</a></sup> Most scholars consider them of dubious authenticity;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsper2010Â§_para._1_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsper2010Â§_para._1-11">[10]</a></sup> Heath in particular contends that the fictionalization was done to strengthen the connection between a revered mathematician and the Arab world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeath1981355_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeath1981355-25">[20]</a></sup> There are also numerous anecdotal stories concerning to Euclid, all of uncertain historicity, which "picture him as a kindly and gentle old man".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991[httpsarchiveorgdetailshistoryofmathema00boyepage100mode2upviewtheater_101]_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991[httpsarchiveorgdetailshistoryofmathema00boyepage100mode2upviewtheater_101]-39">[31]</a></sup> The best known of these is Proclus' story about Ptolemy asking Euclid if there was a quicker path to learning geometry than reading his <i>Elements</i>, which Euclid replied with "there is no royal road to geometry".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991[httpsarchiveorgdetailshistoryofmathema00boyepage100mode2upviewtheater_101]_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991[httpsarchiveorgdetailshistoryofmathema00boyepage100mode2upviewtheater_101]-39">[31]</a></sup> This anecdote is questionable since a very similar interaction between <a href="/wiki/Menaechmus" title="Menaechmus">Menaechmus</a> and Alexander the Great is recorded from <a href="/wiki/Stobaeus" title="Stobaeus">Stobaeus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyer199196_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyer199196-40">[32]</a></sup> Both the accounts were written in the 5th century AD, neither indicate their source, and neither story appears in ancient Greek literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros201890_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros201890-41">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>The traditional narrative of Euclid's activity <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">â300</span> is complicated by no mathematicians of the 4th century BC indicating his existence.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"_6-8'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"-6'>[6]</a></sup> Mathematicians of the 3rd century such as Archimedes and Apollonius "assume a part of his work to be known";<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"_6-9'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"-6'>[6]</a></sup> however, Archimedes strangely uses an older theory of proportions, rather than that of Euclid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsper2010Â§_para._1_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsper2010Â§_para._1-11">[10]</a></sup> The <i>Elements</i> is dated to have been at least partly in circulation by the 3rd century BC.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"_6-10'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"-6'>[6]</a></sup> Some ancient Greek mathematician mention him by name, but he is usually referred to as "á½ ÏÏÎ¿Î¹ÏÎµÎ¹ÏÏÎ·Ï" ("the author of <i>Elements</i>").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeath1981357_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeath1981357-42">[34]</a></sup> In the Middle Ages, some scholars contended Euclid was not a not a historical personage and that his name arose from a corruption of Greek mathematical terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBall1960[httpsarchiveorgdetailsshortaccountofhi0000ballpage52mode2up_52â53]_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBall1960[httpsarchiveorgdetailsshortaccountofhi0000ballpage52mode2up_52â53]-43">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elements"><i>Elements</i></span></h3>
<p><span class="anchor" id="The_''Elements''"></span><span class="anchor" id="Elements"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid's Elements">Euclid's Elements</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" â ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" â ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:300px; ; background-color: #E0E6F8;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p><b>Structure of the <i>Elements</i></b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArtmann20123_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArtmann20123-44">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<hr/>
<dl><dd>Books IâVI: Plane geometry</dd>
<dd>Books VIIâX: Arithmetic</dd>
<dd>Books XIâXIII: Solid geometry</dd></dl>
</blockquote>
</div>
<p>Euclid is best known for his thirteen-book treatise, the <i>Elements</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CF%83%CF%84%CE%BF%CE%B9%CF%87%CE%B5%CE%AF%CE%B1" title="wikt:ÏÏÎ¿Î¹ÏÎµÎ¯Î±">Î£ÏÎ¿Î¹ÏÎµá¿Î±</a></span></span>; <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">Stoicheia</i></span>).<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Summary"_4-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Summary"-4'>[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsper2010Â§_para._2_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsper2010Â§_para._2-45">[37]</a></sup> Although many of the results in the <i>Elements</i> originated with earlier Greek mathematicians, one of Euclid's accomplishments was to present them in a single, logically coherent framework, making it easy to use and easy to reference, including a system of rigorous <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_proof" title="Mathematical proof">mathematical proofs</a> that remains the basis of mathematics 23 centuries later.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStruik1967p._51,_"their_logical_structure_has_influenced_scientific_thinking_perhaps_more_than_any_other_text_in_the_world"_46-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStruik1967p._51,_"their_logical_structure_has_influenced_scientific_thinking_perhaps_more_than_any_other_text_in_the_world"-46'>[38]</a></sup> Among the mathematicians whose work is featured includes Eudoxus, <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates_of_Chios" title="Hippocrates of Chios">Hippocrates of Chios</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thales" title="Thales">Thales</a> and Theaetetus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsper2010Â§_para._6_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsper2010Â§_para._6-47">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although best known for its geometric results, the <i>Elements</i> also includes <a href="/wiki/Number_theory" title="Number theory">number theory</a>. It considers the connection between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perfect_numbers" title="Perfect numbers">perfect numbers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mersenne_primes" title="Mersenne primes">Mersenne primes</a> (known as the <a href="/wiki/Euclid%E2%80%93Euler_theorem" title="EuclidâEuler theorem">EuclidâEuler theorem</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_theorem" title="Euclid's theorem">infinitude of prime numbers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_lemma" title="Euclid's lemma">Euclid's lemma</a> on factorization (which leads to the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_arithmetic" title="Fundamental theorem of arithmetic">fundamental theorem of arithmetic</a> on uniqueness of <a href="/wiki/Integer_factorization" title="Integer factorization">prime factorizations</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_algorithm" title="Euclidean algorithm">Euclidean algorithm</a> for finding the <a href="/wiki/Greatest_common_divisor" title="Greatest common divisor">greatest common divisor</a> of two numbers.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Papyrus_Oxyrhynchus_29" title="Papyrus Oxyrhynchus 29">Papyrus Oxyrhynchus 29</a> (P. Oxy. 29) is a fragment of the second book of the <i><a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid's Elements">Elements</a></i> of Euclid, unearthed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bernard_Grenfell" title="Bernard Grenfell">Grenfell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Surridge_Hunt" title="Arthur Surridge Hunt">Hunt</a> 1897 in <a href="/wiki/Oxyrhynchus" title="Oxyrhynchus">Oxyrhynchus</a>. More recent scholarship suggests a date of 75â125 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler1999210â211_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler1999210â211-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_works">Other works</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Euclid_Dodecahedron_1.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="850" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Euclid_Dodecahedron_1.svg/220px-Euclid_Dodecahedron_1.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Euclid_Dodecahedron_1.svg/330px-Euclid_Dodecahedron_1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Euclid_Dodecahedron_1.svg/440px-Euclid_Dodecahedron_1.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Euclid_Dodecahedron_1.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Euclid's construction of a regular <a href="/wiki/Dodecahedron" title="Dodecahedron">dodecahedron</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In addition to the <i>Elements</i>, at least five works of Euclid have survived to the present day. They follow the same logical structure as <i>Elements</i>, with definitions and proved propositions.
</p>
<ul><li><i>Catoptrics</i> concerns the mathematical theory of mirrors, particularly the images formed in plane and spherical concave mirrors, though the attribution is sometimes questioned.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"_48-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"-48'>[40]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Data_(Euclid)" title="Data (Euclid)">Data</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ÎÎµÎ´Î¿Î¼Î­Î½Î±</span></span>), is a somewhat short text which deals with the nature and implications of "given" information in geometrical problems.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"_48-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"-48'>[40]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>On Divisions</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Î ÎµÏá½¶ ÎÎ¹Î±Î¹ÏÎ­ÏÎµÏÎ½â</span></span>) survives only partially in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> translation, and concerns the division of geometrical figures into two or more equal parts or into parts in given <a href="/wiki/Ratio" title="Ratio">ratios</a>. It includes thirty-six propositions and is similar to Apollonius' <i>Conics</i>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"_48-2'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"-48'>[40]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Optics" title="Euclid's Optics">Optics</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">á½ÏÏÎ¹ÎºÎ¬â</span></span>) is the earliest surviving Greek treatise on perspective. It includes an introductory discussion of <a href="/wiki/Geometrical_optics" title="Geometrical optics">geometrical optics</a> and basic rules of <a href="/wiki/Perspective_(graphical)" title="Perspective (graphical)">perspective</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"_48-3'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"-48'>[40]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <i>Phaenomena</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Î¦Î±Î¹Î½ÏÎ¼ÎµÎ½Î±</span></span>) is a treatise on <a href="/wiki/Spherical_astronomy" title="Spherical astronomy">spherical astronomy</a>, survives in Greek; it is similar to <i>On the Moving Sphere</i> by <a href="/wiki/Autolycus_of_Pitane" title="Autolycus of Pitane">Autolycus of Pitane</a>, who flourished around 310 BC.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"_48-4'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"-48'>[40]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lost_works">Lost works</span></h3>
<p>Four other works are credibly attributed to Euclid, but have been lost.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Works"_12-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Works"-12'>[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The <i>Conics</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ÎÏÎ½Î¹ÎºÎ¬â</span></span>) was a four-book survey on <a href="/wiki/Conic_section" title="Conic section">conic sections</a>, which was later superseded by a Apollonius' more comprehensive treatment of the same name.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaisbakWaerden2021Â§_"Other_writings"_49-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaisbakWaerden2021Â§_"Other_writings"-49'>[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"_48-5'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"-48'>[40]</a></sup> The work's existence is known primarily from Pappus, who asserts that the first four books of Apollonius' <i>Conics</i> are largely based on Euclid's earlier work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones1986399â400_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones1986399â400-50">[42]</a></sup> Doubt has been cast on this assertion by the historian <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Alexander_Jones_(Wissenschaftshistoriker)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Alexander Jones (Wissenschaftshistoriker) (page does not exist)">Alexander Jones</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">Â [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_Jones_(Wissenschaftshistoriker)" title="de:Alexander Jones (Wissenschaftshistoriker)">de</a>]</span>, owing to sparse evidence and no other corroboration of Pappus' account.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones1986399â400_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones1986399â400-50">[42]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <i>Pseudaria</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Î¨ÎµÏÎ´Î¬ÏÎ¹Î±â</span></span>; <abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;">â</span>'Fallacies'), wasâaccording to Proclus in (70.1â18)âa text in geometrical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a>, written to advise beginners in avoiding common fallacies.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaisbakWaerden2021Â§_"Other_writings"_49-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaisbakWaerden2021Â§_"Other_writings"-49'>[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"_48-6'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"-48'>[40]</a></sup> Very little is known of its specific contents aside from its scope and a few extant lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAcerbi2008511_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAcerbi2008511-51">[43]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <i>Porisms</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Î Î¿ÏÎ¯ÏÎ¼Î±ÏÎ±</span></span>; <abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;">â</span>'Corollaries') was, based on accounts from Pappus and Proclus, probably a three-book treatise with approximately 200 propositions.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaisbakWaerden2021Â§_"Other_writings"_49-2'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaisbakWaerden2021Â§_"Other_writings"-49'>[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"_48-7'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"-48'>[40]</a></sup> The term '<a href="/wiki/Porism" title="Porism">porism</a>' in this context does not refer to a <a href="/wiki/Corollary" title="Corollary">corollary</a>, but to "a third type of propositionâan intermediate between a theorem and a problemâthe aim of which is to discover a feature of an existing geometrical entity, for example, to find the centre of a circle".<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"_48-8'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"-48'>[40]</a></sup> The mathematician <a href="/wiki/Michel_Chasles" title="Michel Chasles">Michel Chasles</a> speculated that these now-lost propositions included content related to the modern theories of <a href="/wiki/Transversal_(geometry)" title="Transversal (geometry)">transversals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Projective_geometry" title="Projective geometry">projective geometry</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaisbakWaerden2021Â§_"Other_writings"_49-3'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaisbakWaerden2021Â§_"Other_writings"-49'>[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[i]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <i>Surface Loci</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Î¤ÏÏÎ¿Î¹ ÏÏá½¸Ï á¼ÏÎ¹ÏÎ±Î½ÎµÎ¯á¾³</span></span>) is of virtually unknown contents, aside from speculation based on the work's title.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaisbakWaerden2021Â§_"Other_writings"_49-4'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaisbakWaerden2021Â§_"Other_writings"-49'>[41]</a></sup> Conjecture based on later accounts has suggested it discussed cones and cylinders, among other subjects.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"_48-9'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Other_Works"-48'>[40]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_things_named_after_Euclid" title="List of things named after Euclid">List of things named after Euclid</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byrne1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="688" data-file-width="543" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Byrne1.png/170px-Byrne1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Byrne1.png/255px-Byrne1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Byrne1.png/340px-Byrne1.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Byrne1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The cover page of <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Byrne_(mathematician)" title="Oliver Byrne (mathematician)">Oliver Byrne</a>'s 1847 colored edition of the <i>Elements</i></div></div></div>
<p>Euclid is generally considered with Archimedes and Apollonius of Perga as among the greatest mathematicians of antiquity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBall1960[httpsarchiveorgdetailsshortaccountofhi0000ballpage52mode2up_52]_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBall1960[httpsarchiveorgdetailsshortaccountofhi0000ballpage52mode2up_52]-19">[15]</a></sup> Many commentators cite him as one of the most influential figures in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_mathematics" title="History of mathematics">history of mathematics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage125_125]_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage125_125]-3">[3]</a></sup> The geometrical system established by the <i>Elements</i> long dominated the field; however, today that system is often referred to as '<a href="/wiki/Euclidean_geometry" title="Euclidean geometry">Euclidean geometry</a>' to distinguish it from other <a href="/wiki/Non-Euclidean_geometry" title="Non-Euclidean geometry">non-Euclidean geometries</a> discovered in the early 19th century.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaisbakWaerden2021Â§_"Legacy"_53-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaisbakWaerden2021Â§_"Legacy"-53'>[44]</a></sup> Among Euclid's <a href="/wiki/List_of_things_named_after_Euclid" title="List of things named after Euclid">many namesakes</a> are the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a>'s (ESA) <a href="/wiki/Euclid_(spacecraft)" title="Euclid (spacecraft)">Euclid</a> spacecraft,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[45]</a></sup> the lunar crater <a href="/wiki/Euclides_(crater)" title="Euclides (crater)">Euclides</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[46]</a></sup> and the minor planet <a href="/wiki/4354_Euclides" title="4354 Euclides">4354 Euclides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Elements</i> is often considered after the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> as the most frequently translated, published, and studied book in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_World" title="Western World">Western World</a>'s history.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaisbakWaerden2021Â§_"Legacy"_53-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaisbakWaerden2021Â§_"Legacy"-53'>[44]</a></sup> With Aristotle's <i><a href="/wiki/Metaphysics_(Aristotle)" title="Metaphysics (Aristotle)">Metaphysics</a></i>, the <i>Elements</i> is perhaps the most successful ancient Greek text, and was the dominant mathematical textbook in the Medieval Arab and Latin worlds.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaisbakWaerden2021Â§_"Legacy"_53-2'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaisbakWaerden2021Â§_"Legacy"-53'>[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first English edition of the <i>Elements</i> was published in 1570 by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Billingsley" title="Henry Billingsley">Henry Billingsley</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Dee" title="John Dee">John Dee</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoulding2010120_35-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoulding2010120-35">[29]</a></sup> The mathematician <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Byrne_(mathematician)" title="Oliver Byrne (mathematician)">Oliver Byrne</a> published a well-known version of the <i>Elements</i>  in 1847 entitled <i>The First Six Books of the Elements of Euclid in Which Coloured Diagrams and Symbols Are Used Instead of Letters for the Greater Ease of Learners</i>, which included colored diagrams intended to increase its pedagogical effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_Hilbert" title="David Hilbert">David Hilbert</a> authored a <a href="/wiki/Hilbert%27s_axioms" title="Hilbert's axioms">modern axiomatization</a> of the <i>Elements</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHÃ¤hlPeters2022Â§_para._1_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHÃ¤hlPeters2022Â§_para._1-58">[49]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Later Arab sources state he was a Greek born in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Tyre, Lebanon</a>, though these accounts are considered dubious and speculative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsper2010Â§_para._1_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsper2010Â§_para._1-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"_6-3'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"-6'>[6]</a></sup> See <a href="#CITEREFHeath1981">Heath 1981</a>, p.Â 355 for an English translation of the Arab account. He was long held to have been born in Megara, but by the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> it was concluded that he had been confused with the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Euclid_of_Megara" title="Euclid of Megara">Euclid of Megara</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoulding2010118_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoulding2010118-23">[19]</a></sup> see <a href="#Identity_and_historicity">Â§Identity and historicity</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Musaeum" title="Musaeum">Musaeum</a> would later include the famous <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Alexandria" title="Library of Alexandria">Library of Alexandria</a>, but it was likely founded later, during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_II_Philadelphus" title="Ptolemy II Philadelphus">Ptolemy II Philadelphus</a> (285â246 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy2000343â344_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy2000343â344-32">[27]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The historian Robert Goulding notes that the "common conflation of Euclid of Megara and Euclid the mathematician in Byzantine sources" suggests that doing so was a "more extensive tradition" than just the account of Valerius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoulding2010120_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoulding2010120-35">[29]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This misidentification also appeared in Art; the 17th-century painting <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">Euclide di Megara si traveste da donna per recarsi ad Atene a seguire le lezioni di Socrate</i></span> [<i>Euclid of Megara Dressing as a Woman to Hear Socrates Teach in Athens</i>] by <a href="/wiki/Domenico_Maroli" title="Domenico Maroli">Domenico Maroli</a> portrays the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Euclid_of_Megara" title="Euclid of Megara">Euclid of Megara</a> but includes mathematical objects on his desk, under the false impression that he is also Euclid of Alexandria.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"_and_Note_5_37-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTESialaros2021Â§_"Life"_and_Note_5-37'>[30]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-52">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See <a href="#CITEREFJones1986">Jones 1986</a>, pp.Â 547â572 for further information on the <i>Porisms</i></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
